<div id="review_53629153" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/53629153" />
    <a title="Leslie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/115525-leslie"><img alt="Leslie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1296854168p2/115525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/53629153?book_show_action=true">Apr 22, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leslie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leslie" href="/user/show/115525-leslie">Leslie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/115525-leslie?shelf=one-sitting-reads">one-sitting-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer53629153" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16864365740805871629">One of my literary pet peeves: writers writing about their mental illnesses. I avoid books like this one, largely because I believe the cult of romanticism surrounding artistic despair is misguided to the point of being offensive. It reminds me of being stuck in an undergraduate seminar with that girl who wore black eyeliner and too many bracelets, lugged around conspicuous copies of Plath and Sexton, and wrote bad poems about her sex life. As both a writer and someone who suffers from chronic d</span>
  <span id="freeText16864365740805871629" style="display:none">One of my literary pet peeves: writers writing about their mental illnesses. I avoid books like this one, largely because I believe the cult of romanticism surrounding artistic despair is misguided to the point of being offensive. It reminds me of being stuck in an undergraduate seminar with that girl who wore black eyeliner and too many bracelets, lugged around conspicuous copies of Plath and Sexton, and wrote bad poems about her sex life. As both a writer and someone who suffers from chronic depression, I see nothing romantic about the myth of the suffering artist. (It also angers me that many people assume the two facets of my life depend on each other: I suffer, so I write. I write, so I suffer. No. I'm depressed because I come from a long line of depressives, and I write because I want to, and I take it seriously, not as some form of mad exorcism or touchy-feely therapy.) So the idea that I - or anyone - would write about their own suicidal tendencies seems both cliched and dangerously misleading. However, I was moved by Styron's pithy and unsentimental account of his battle with depression - largely because he didn't connect to any "artistic" sensibility at all. It's a slim volume, but a large piece of it was devoted to his utter <i>bewilderment</i>. His depression set in during middle age, when he was settled, married, and working. It's not something that burned in him like "the fire of inspiration" - it was frightening, origin-less, and eventually incapacitating. This is something I responded to - depression is an irrational, lonely, very physical disease, and not always connected to the grandiosity of one's ego, or the peak of one's art. I got a little choked up at the end, with the Dante quotes. So true.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16864365740805871629" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText13057040034481753829" style="display:none">This was planned to be a buddy read with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com" rel="nofollow noopener">https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/24783204-ali</a><br />but I ended up finishing it before he could even start it.. <div id="review_2200700533" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2200700533" />
    <a title="zainab_booklover" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28880190-zainab-booklover"><img alt="zainab_booklover" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451660719p2/28880190.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2200700533?book_show_action=true">Dec 01, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="zainab_booklover" class="user" itemprop="url" name="zainab_booklover" href="/user/show/28880190-zainab-booklover">zainab_booklover</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer16164956481039868537">In <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/36505581.Darkness_Visible_A_Memoir_of_Madness' title='Darkness Visible A Memoir of Madness by William Styron' rel='nofollow noopener'>Darkness Visible: A Memoir of Madness</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7565.William_Styron' title='William Styron' rel='nofollow noopener'>William Styron</a> endeavours to describe the undescribable. You have to read it to fully comprehend its importance and significance. Thus, it is definitely a must-read for everyone! <br/><br/><i>''Depression, most people know, used to be termed "melancholia," a word which appears in English as early as the year 1303 and crops up more than once in Chaucer, who in his usage seemed to be aware of its pathological nuances.<br/><br/>"Melancholia" would still appear to be a far more ap</i></span>
  <span id="freeText16164956481039868537" style="display:none">In <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/36505581.Darkness_Visible_A_Memoir_of_Madness" title="Darkness Visible A Memoir of Madness by William Styron" rel="nofollow noopener">Darkness Visible: A Memoir of Madness</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7565.William_Styron" title="William Styron" rel="nofollow noopener">William Styron</a> endeavours to describe the undescribable. You have to read it to fully comprehend its importance and significance. Thus, it is definitely a must-read for everyone! <br /><br /><i>''Depression, most people know, used to be termed "melancholia," a word which appears in English as early as the year 1303 and crops up more than once in Chaucer, who in his usage seemed to be aware of its pathological nuances.<br /><br />"Melancholia" would still appear to be a far more apt and evocative word for the blacker forms of the disorder, but it was usurped by a noun with a bland tonality and lacking any magisterial presence, used indifferently to describe an economic decline or a rut in the ground, a true wimp of a word for such a major illness.'' <i><br /><br /><b>''Depression afflicts millions directly, and millions more who are relatives or friends of victims. It has been estimated that as many as one in ten Americans will suffer from the illness. As assertively democratic as a Norman Rockwell poster, it strikes indiscriminately at all ages, races, creeds and classes, though women are at considerably higher risk than men. [....] it is enough to say that very few people escape being a potential victim of the disease, at least in its milder form.''<b></b></b></i></i></span>

<div id="review_14267111" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/14267111" />
    <a title="Kristen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/834858-kristen"><img alt="Kristen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1206529307p2/834858.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/14267111?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kristen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kristen" href="/user/show/834858-kristen">Kristen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer14267111" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1186471223851654716">At a recent tenure party, a friend of mine leaned over to our small group sitting on the couch and revealed that she had just come from the campus bookstore where she had been perusing a colleague’s recent memoir. “I would never expose myself like that!” she exclaimed. When writers choose to invade their own privacy, as Styron puts it, by sharing a personal struggle, is that what they’re doing—exposing themselves? Certainly, on some level, when Styron sets his struggle with suicidal depression i</span>
  <span id="freeText1186471223851654716" style="display:none">At a recent tenure party, a friend of mine leaned over to our small group sitting on the couch and revealed that she had just come from the campus bookstore where she had been perusing a colleague’s recent memoir. “I would never expose myself like that!” she exclaimed. When writers choose to invade their own privacy, as Styron puts it, by sharing a personal struggle, is that what they’re doing—exposing themselves? Certainly, on some level, when Styron sets his struggle with suicidal depression in print, he is. He does so knowing that many of the people who read his book may likely find him “different,” and maybe even judge him to be weak or crazy or both. They will set him aside in their minds as an “other.” And in a way, isn’t this exactly what my friend was implying with her exclamation—that she doesn’t want everyone to know all the ways, big and small, that she is different or doesn’t “belong”? Yet, what my friend is missing is that a writer like Styron isn’t engaged in a reckless act of self-exhibitionism; he is engaged in a project of cultural critique. He forges ahead with his story despite the socialized impulse to keep hidden what we are taught to experience as shameful or “abnormal.” His book is an act of faith and a form of resistance. He must trust that readers who have never experienced suicidal depression will find points of connection with him as well as points of difference, thereby experiencing the heretofore unfamiliar as familiar. His book becomes like a stone dropped into a still pond. It will resonate most profoundly with those who share his struggles, but its resonance will expand out to touch others. His sharing of his account strikes me not as an act of shocking self-exposure but rather as a brave act of generosity. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1186471223851654716" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText3009113969928195498" style="display:none">“The disease of depression remains a great mystery. It has yielded its secrets to science far more reluctantly than many of the other major ills besetting us.”<br /><br />“Mysterious in its coming, mysterious in its going, the affliction runs its course, and one finds peace.”<br /><br />William Styron’s Darkness Visible is an inspiring, informative and well-written memoir. This short book provides an in-depth understanding and vivid portrayal of the dark and painful phase and “those states of excruciating near-paralysis” that a person with mental depression and emotional illness goes through. <br /><br />“Men and women who have recovered from the disease -- and they are countless – bear witness to what is probably its only saving grace: <i>it is conquerable. </i>”<br /><br />But, more than anything, this memoir is about the fact that even though it feels next to impossible, one can recover from this phase. <br /><br /><b>”And so we came forth, and once again beheld the stars.”</b> <br /></span>

<div id="review_145199397" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/145199397" />
    <a title="Tara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3056485-tara"><img alt="Tara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1473769566p2/3056485.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/145199397?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tara" href="/user/show/3056485-tara">Tara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3056485-tara?shelf=biography-memoir">biography-memoir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3056485-tara?shelf=mental-illness">mental-illness</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer145199397" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7729493470251006999">Given the number of great reviews this book had, I was eager to read, especially regarding a topic I feel is extremely neglected in good literature. Having experienced this 'darkness' without remittance for most of my life, I had high hopes for this book- which he did deliver, and evident in his descriptions of feeling like a 'husk', and the fragile moments following a near-suicide attempt-<br/><br/> "this sound, which like all music- indeed like all pleasure- I had been numbly unresponsive to for months,</span>
  <span id="freeText7729493470251006999" style="display:none">Given the number of great reviews this book had, I was eager to read, especially regarding a topic I feel is extremely neglected in good literature. Having experienced this 'darkness' without remittance for most of my life, I had high hopes for this book- which he did deliver, and evident in his descriptions of feeling like a 'husk', and the fragile moments following a near-suicide attempt-<br /><br /> "this sound, which like all music- indeed like all pleasure- I had been numbly unresponsive to for months, pierced my heart like a dagger, and in a flood of swift recollection I thought of all the joys the house had known.... All this I realized was more than I could inflict on these memories, and upon those, so close to me, with whom those memories were bound. And just as powerfully I realized I could not commit this desecration on myself. I drew upon some last gleam of sanity to perceive the terrifying dimensions of the mortal predicament I had fallen into."<br /><br />Styron is the first writer I have read whom I feel portrays depression accurately, stripping it of it's stigma and shame, and explains the rawness in all it's haunting emptiness.<br /><br />Rather than it's downfall, the briefness of this book was perfect, as it meant that even one with difficulty concentrating could read and comprehend the text without feeling overwhelmed.<br /><br />This book was great, the only downfall, I would have liked to have read more! Also, I would have loved more expansion on the rawness of ones' feelings, especially given the considerable length he uses to instill the possibility of recovery, which I felt too difficult to comprehend given my current state of mind. Overall, a highly recommended book for anyone, those who have depression and those who wish to have a glimpse of depressions' insidiousness.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7729493470251006999" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2368262595" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2368262595" />
    <a title="Celia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30261451-celia"><img alt="Celia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1466854747p2/30261451.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2368262595?book_show_action=true">May 16, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Celia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Celia" href="/user/show/30261451-celia">Celia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30261451-celia?shelf=bios-memoirs">bios-memoirs</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30261451-celia?shelf=2018-read">2018-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30261451-celia?shelf=jaline">jaline</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2368262595" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8884905538672066051">William Styron is a beloved author having written such classics as The Confessions of Nat Turner and Sophie's Choice.<br/><br/>I read 'Nat' soon after it was published (1967) and neither remember it or this author's wonderful writing style.<br/><br/>Needless to say I knew nothing about his descent into a very dark depression. This book chronicles that descent. At first I was nervous about reading this book because my mother was bipolar, my paternal grandfather suffered from serious depression, and my sister died be</span>
  <span id="freeText8884905538672066051" style="display:none">William Styron is a beloved author having written such classics as The Confessions of Nat Turner and Sophie's Choice.<br /><br />I read 'Nat' soon after it was published (1967) and neither remember it or this author's wonderful writing style.<br /><br />Needless to say I knew nothing about his descent into a very dark depression. This book chronicles that descent. At first I was nervous about reading this book because my mother was bipolar, my paternal grandfather suffered from serious depression, and my sister died because of her depression, taking her own life. <br /><br />I need not have been afraid. Styron wrote about his experience almost lovingly. Never did he portray the attitude of 'why me' or write the words of a martyr. <br /><br />I am so sorry that Styron had to endure this but so glad he lived to write about it and share it with us.<br /><br />His ultimate stay in a very good hospital for 7 weeks effected the cure he needed. I was especially impressed with his description of his hospital stay. That it was a place of peace where he could start to heal.<br /><br />There are many other lessons to be learned from this book. Depression was a very big influence on my family and I am glad I read this special book. <br /><br />Next I need to reread 'Nat' and read Sophie's Choice and his first book, Lie Down in Darkness (1951).<br /><br />5 stars</span>
  <a data-text-id="8884905538672066051" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2039969021" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2039969021" />
    <a title="Jennifer Gaarder" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32165901-jennifer-gaarder"><img alt="Jennifer Gaarder" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1558144433p2/32165901.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2039969021?book_show_action=true">Jun 25, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jennifer Gaarder" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jennifer Gaarder" href="/user/show/32165901-jennifer-gaarder">Jennifer Gaarder</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2039969021" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6132296788549349361">Read my reviews at jenchaosreviews.com<br/><br/>Darkness Visible By William Styron<br/><br/>Vintage, January 8, 1992<br/><br/>82 Pages, Paperback Edition<br/><br/><br/><br/>From Goodreads:<br/><br/>"A work of great personal courage and a literary tour de force, this bestseller is Styron's true account of his descent into a crippling and almost suicidal depression. Styron is perhaps the first writer to convey the full terror of depression's psychic landscape, as well as the illuminating path to recovery."<br/><br/><br/><br/>Goodreads Rating: 4.04/ My Rating: 5.00<br/><br/><br/><br/>Review:<br/>Among</span>
  <span id="freeText6132296788549349361" style="display:none">Read my reviews at jenchaosreviews.com<br /><br />Darkness Visible By William Styron<br /><br />Vintage, January 8, 1992<br /><br />82 Pages, Paperback Edition<br /><br /><br /><br />From Goodreads:<br /><br />"A work of great personal courage and a literary tour de force, this bestseller is Styron's true account of his descent into a crippling and almost suicidal depression. Styron is perhaps the first writer to convey the full terror of depression's psychic landscape, as well as the illuminating path to recovery."<br /><br /><br /><br />Goodreads Rating: 4.04/ My Rating: 5.00<br /><br /><br /><br />Review:<br />Among the many memoirs of madness that I have read, this one happens to be one of the best. Written with presence, William Styron is open and honest about the pain and suffering that goes along with clinical depression in a way that I have never seen before in any other memoir.<br /><br />While reading this, I felt that this particular writer "got it" as far as the symptoms of deep depression go. Going through something similar myself, this was something I read to make myself understand that I am not alone and that people have been able to recover and go on with their lives.<br /><br />At the age of 60, William Styron, famed writer, author of many books including Sophie's Choice and many others, fell victim to a broad and miserable depression that almost took his life. While undergoing treatment, he fell under the spell of the controversial benzodiazepine Halcyon and was told that he could take it "any time he like, as much as he like." Without realizing the horror that comes with taking depressants, he was forced to another doctor with unsuccessful results.<br /><br />Because this happened at an earlier time (the 1980s), the treatments for depression were not well refined as they are now in this day of 2019. He was given a host of the day's antidepressants including Nardil. Fearing the worst, he felt no better.<br /><br />While reading this, I became more and more depressed. He wove such a tale of weakness and madness that I thought that there is no help for "crazy people" like myself. However, he finally relented and went to a hospital where they did stabilize his mood. He knew that he had a terrible disorder and also knew that once a person "gets" depression, it is likely to come again.<br /><br />William Styron was more than honest in this short memoir. Darkness Visible is written for someone who is suffering and contemplating suicide as he did. What stopped him is something that stopped me from the act. In this life, we find that family, memories and yes, our children deserve better. This and all of the other things that go along with life are what stops a person from committing suicide.<br /><br />While reading this, he referred to depression as a "disease," which it is not. Depression, like all mental illness, is a disorder of the brain/mind. Disease denotes some viral or bacterial infection. He also referred to depressive spells as "seizures." Seizures are a rather complex and serious thing. They happen when neurons overreact in one part of the brain and cause a person to "seize."<br /><br />This is all because it was written at a time when things were less known about mental disorders, and it was considered a faux pas to have them. Likewise, most mental disorders like manic depression (bipolar) or depressive illness would be acceptable to women and not successful men. This would explain why Mr. Styron tried so hard NOT to go to the hospital when he needed to.<br /><br />Writing:<br />Mr. Styron employs a classical method of writing anything. In his stories and indeed this book, the words are paired together in musical harmony. This makes a depressing and horrible book seem less horrible. The act of writing down something so terrible is painful enough, but to be able to do it with such grace is something of a marvel. This is why he was an award-winning writer.<br /><br />Objective:<br />Because this is a memoir, there really is no PLOT, there does seem to be an objective, however. This book serves as an information piece for people who want to know what it is like to have depression, truly. Also, this serves as a book of hope. Yes, there is a light at the end of the long, dark tunnel. Stay the course, follow through with treatment and you too can get out of the mirk.<br /><br />What I Liked:<br />The honesty of such a horrible disease was what impressed me the most. A man who is celebrated for his creative talent and wonderful writing is suffering from one of the worst types of mental illness? This is what makes me feel that not just "regular" people get attacked with these things. This is what people in my and many other's position need.<br /><br />What I Didn't Like:<br />I did not like that he referred to depression as a disease. This is a disorder like all mental illnesses. It sort of irritated me. I know that the CDC ( Centers For Disease Control) and NIH( National Institute of Health) are looking at depression as a worldwide problem, but it is not a disease, in that you can not contract it from another person.<br /><br />Overall Impression:<br />Darkness Visible is the most honest you will get from an award-winning author about the mental disorder depression. Symptoms, treatments, suicide ideation and eventual hospitalization are some of the things discussed. This is a great book if you are looking for answers or something to inform yourself. I rated this book a 5.00.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6132296788549349361" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_210246683" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/210246683" />
    <a title="Sam Quixote" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5759543-sam-quixote"><img alt="Sam Quixote" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1529183438p2/5759543.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/210246683?book_show_action=true">Sep 18, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sam Quixote" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sam Quixote" href="/user/show/5759543-sam-quixote">Sam Quixote</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer210246683" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6513207927398827512">I picked read this as I'm always interested in peoples' experiences with depression and how they deal with it/emerge from it, as well as how it was for them. I think sometimes I'm depressed but having read this book I think what I have might simply be the occasional blues. <br/><br/>William Styron makes this distinction clear in his memoir "Darkness Visible" where he says that full on depression (a term he deplores as too weak a description - he prefers the label "brainstorm") totally cripples a person. T</span>
  <span id="freeText6513207927398827512" style="display:none">I picked read this as I'm always interested in peoples' experiences with depression and how they deal with it/emerge from it, as well as how it was for them. I think sometimes I'm depressed but having read this book I think what I have might simply be the occasional blues. <br /><br />William Styron makes this distinction clear in his memoir "Darkness Visible" where he says that full on depression (a term he deplores as too weak a description - he prefers the label "brainstorm") totally cripples a person. They're unable to do anything, can't get through a day, and contemplating death becomes almost pleasurable as it's an escape from their condition. <br /><br />I think this was the best thing to come out of reading this book - the understanding between being unhappy with yourself and your life sometimes but still continuing and taking comfort from little things, to being clinically depressed. While a lack of action and a blackness in thought were the things I took from this as being clinically depressed, I never really felt that I understood what it was like to be this way. The best Styron manages is a sort of lethargy that most people can relate to, he can never actually fully articulate the sensation (or lack thereof) of depression (odd as he is a very verbose writer). <br /><br />A lot of the book is taken up with Styron musing on other famous writers who were depressed as well as the treatment of his depression - nothing particularly special, pills, followed by hospitalisation and counselling. <br /><br />Though it's a short book, I felt Styron didn't quite put across the experience of depression very well in the book and I came away from it none the wiser having read it. "Prozac Nation" is a less well written but more enlightening read by someone who's also been through the wringer of mental hell. "Darkness Visible" felt like it was written by someone afraid, or unable, to return back to the depths to dredge up the experience on paper and so produced a weaker book as a result. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6513207927398827512" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2999719735" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2999719735" />
    <a title="Steve" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48011083-steve"><img alt="Steve" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1542289434p2/48011083.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2999719735?book_show_action=true">Oct 03, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steve" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steve" href="/user/show/48011083-steve">Steve</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2999719735" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9442412653856849742">Mr. Styron’s work is notable because he lends his skill as a professional writer to the task of describing a nebulous malady that effects so many, yet defies adequate description; here he largely succeeds, with an important qualification. I’m interested in his account because I have suffered depression’s influence, to various degrees, throughout my adult life. While I believe I’m a functional depressive, often, surprisingly, highly functional, there have been stretches where I’ve been laid might</span>
  <span id="freeText9442412653856849742" style="display:none">Mr. Styron’s work is notable because he lends his skill as a professional writer to the task of describing a nebulous malady that effects so many, yet defies adequate description; here he largely succeeds, with an important qualification. I’m interested in his account because I have suffered depression’s influence, to various degrees, throughout my adult life. While I believe I’m a functional depressive, often, surprisingly, highly functional, there have been stretches where I’ve been laid mighty low, those nadirs now seeming to coincide with periods of mental idleness, which, unfortunately, have proven far too frequent and lengthy in recent decades.<br /><br />At its heart, Mr. Styron attempts to communicate a sense of understanding, a sense of comprehension for this feeling. Often in life, this seems easy enough. If I say, “I’m cold,” or “I’m hot,” I believe you know exactly what I mean. I think it’s fair to say you <i>do</i> know what I mean since you, too, have often been hot or cold. However, if I say, “I’m depressed,” I’m not sure you understand what’s going on in my head; further, you probably <i>do not</i> really understand, especially if you’ve never experienced identical symptoms. Put another way, try describing what salt tastes like and you can get an idea of the communication frustration.<br /><br />The words that do come to mind from my experience, though imperfect, include: <blockquote>intolerable, agony, lethargy, inertia, lost, disconsolate, debilitating, torment, despair, melancholy, distress, absence of {energy, meaning, self-esteem, thought, desire, pleasure}, self-loathing, self-criticism, self-hatred, self-destruction, shame, hopelessness, desolate, forlorn, alone.</blockquote> Maybe I should also add the word suffering; though I’m a bit unsure of that one. One word I would not include is pain, a word Mr. Styron uses often. For me, pain is a toothache or a backache or a smashed finger. Depression, to me, rather, seems an absence of pain, frequently an absence of anything, a vacuum.<br /><br />While Mr. Styron did bring me into his depressive realm with honesty and authenticity, I found the ending unbelievable; as if merely by walking through a revolving door he shook his nagging demon. Unlike many ailments, I find depression a stubborn, chronic condition, forever defying simple solution, including the pharmacological ones, a condition that will be there tomorrow, and the day after that, and the day after that, and the day after that. And yet I keep going, occasionally dwelling on Emily Dickinson’s “’Hope’ is the thing with feathers ….” The other alternative while sometimes enticingly front of mind, is, in the end, impossible.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9442412653856849742" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1719870268" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1719870268" />
    <a title="Melora" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5586131-melora"><img alt="Melora" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548110965p2/5586131.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1719870268?book_show_action=true">Aug 05, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melora" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melora" href="/user/show/5586131-melora">Melora</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780394588889" href="/book/show/930226.Darkness_Visible">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1719870268" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10392249160433694076">Styron's memoir of his descent into severe depression, brief (eighty-four pages) though it is, is powerful and absorbing. He chronicles his illness, from the point where he recognized the seriousness of his situation, through months of increasing despair, darkness and blank, helpless dependency, and then, after crisis, to wellness. His thoughts on what may have triggered and exacerbated his illness are interesting, though, as he notes, depression is very idiosyncratic. The recognition that some </span>
  <span id="freeText10392249160433694076" style="display:none">Styron's memoir of his descent into severe depression, brief (eighty-four pages) though it is, is powerful and absorbing. He chronicles his illness, from the point where he recognized the seriousness of his situation, through months of increasing despair, darkness and blank, helpless dependency, and then, after crisis, to wellness. His thoughts on what may have triggered and exacerbated his illness are interesting, though, as he notes, depression is very idiosyncratic. The recognition that some doctors can be astonishingly cavalier in prescribing sedatives and other mood-altering drugs (though I think this has changed somewhat since Styron's book was published in 1990) is a good one to keep in mind, at any rate.<br /><br />His tone is restrained, sometimes bordering on the clinical, but he can also be quite funny, in an ironic and self-deprecating way. For example... <br /><blockquote>”That afternoon I had been driven (I could no longer drive) to Dr. Gold's office, where he announced that he had decided to place me on the antidepressant Nardil, an older medication which had the advantage of not causing the urinary retention of the other two pills he had prescribed. However, there were drawbacks. Nardil would probably not take effect in less than four to six weeks – I could scarcely believe this – and I would have to carefully obey certain dietary restrictions, fortunately rather epicurean (no sausage, no cheese, no pate de foie gras), in order to avoid a class of incompatible enzymes that might cause a stroke. Further, Dr. Gold said with a straight face, the pill at optimum dosage could have the side effect of impotence. Until that moment, although I'd had some trouble with his personality, I had not thought him totally lacking in perspicacity; now I was not at all sure. Putting myself in Dr. Gold's shoes, I wondered if he seriously thought that this juiceless and ravaged semi-invalid with the shuffle and the ancient wheeze woke up each morning from his Halcion sleep eager for carnal fun.”</blockquote><br /><br />I expect that a fair percentage of the people who pick up this book do so in the hope that Styron will help them articulate what is going on with the glooms they themselves or a loved one are, to one extent or other, suffering through. They have asked or been asked, “what is wrong?” and “what can I do to help?” and the only answer is “nothing” and “nothing.” But Styron can offer only very limited illumination. When even such a fine writer can't convey the experience of depression, I suppose one can assume it can't really be done. One is still left with the same vague mutterings about blackness pressing suffocatingly in on one's brain, and, at the same time, the mind spinning madly into the void. Which, not surprisingly, fails to convey anything useful. But, there it is. Still, misery loves company and all that, and the success of Styron's memoir certainly does offer the assurance that, however alone one <i>feels</i>, many others have tottered along this path.<br /><br />The last pages are rather lovely.<br /><blockquote>”Since antiquity – in the tortured lament of Job, in the choruses of Sophocles and Aeschylus – chroniclers of the human spirit have been wrestling with a vocabulary that might give proper expression to the desolation of melancholia. Through the course of literature and art the theme of depression has run like a durable thread of woe – from Hamlet's soliloquy to the verses of Emily Dickinson and Gerard Manley Hopkins, from John Donne to Hawthorne and Dostoevski and Poe, Camus and Conrad and Virginia Woolf. In many of Albrecht Durer's engravings there are harrowing depictions of his own melancholia; the manic wheeling stars of Van Gogh are the precursors of the artist's plunge into dementia and the extinction of self. It is a suffering that often tinges the music of Beethoven, of Shumann and Mahler, and permeates the darker cantatas of Bach. The vast metaphor which most faithfully represents this fathomless ordeal, however, is that of Dante, and his all-too-familiar lines still arrest the imagination with their augury of the unknowable, the black struggle to come:<br />...<br />'In the middle of the journey of our life<br />I found myself in a dark wood,<br />For I had lost the right path.'<br /><br /> One can be sure that these words have been more than once employed to conjure the ravages of melancholia, but their somber foreboding has often overshadowed the last lines of the best-known part of that poem, with their evocation of hope. To most of those who have experienced it, the horror of depression is so overwhelming as to be quite beyond expression, hence the frustrated sense of inadequacy found in the work of even the greatest artists... But one need not sound the false or inspirational note to stress the truth that depression is not the soul's annihilation; men and women who have recovered from the disease – and they are countless – bear witness to what is probably its only saving grace: it is conquerable.<br /> For those who have dwelt in depression's dark wood, and known its inexplicable agony, their return from the abyss is not unlike the ascent of the poet, trudging upward and upward out of hell's black depths and at last emerging into what he saw as “the shining world.” There, whoever has been restored to health has almost always been restored to the capacity for serenity and joy, and this may be indemnity enough for having endured the despair beyond despair.<br />…<br />'<i>And so we came forth, and once again beheld the stars.'</i></blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="10392249160433694076" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_2790548429" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2790548429" />
    <a title="El" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83144-el"><img alt="El" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1479156984p2/83144.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2790548429?book_show_action=true">Apr 18, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="El" class="user" itemprop="url" name="El" href="/user/show/83144-el">El</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to El by: </span>
      Brian Leyden
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=20th-centurylit-late">20th-centurylit-late</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=mfa">mfa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=science-and-medicine">science-and-medicine</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=library-borrow">library-borrow</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2790548429" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16904896118036445537">I started reading this on a chartered Greyhound bus with like 23 undergraduate students traveling back to Pittsburgh from Manhattan overnight which, in retrospect, is probably the wrong time to read some William Styron.<br/><br/>In the mid-1980s, Styron first realized that he had spiraled into a nearly suicidal depression. There are statistics out there about depression - the number of people who suffer from depression, as one example - so I don't feel the need to repeat any of them here. Studies have bee</span>
  <span id="freeText16904896118036445537" style="display:none">I started reading this on a chartered Greyhound bus with like 23 undergraduate students traveling back to Pittsburgh from Manhattan overnight which, in retrospect, is probably the wrong time to read some William Styron.<br /><br />In the mid-1980s, Styron first realized that he had spiraled into a nearly suicidal depression. There are statistics out there about depression - the number of people who suffer from depression, as one example - so I don't feel the need to repeat any of them here. Studies have been done. Research is constantly being made into the effects of depression - what it does to the brain and the body, what is going on in the brain and the body to cause it, are there genetic issues, environmental concerns, etc. etc. Are more people suffering from depression today than they were in the mid-1980s, for example.<br /><br />While it's not unusual for someone to suffer from depression, even such a paralyzing and debilitating depression as Styron suffered, what he managed to do with it is rather remarkable. He looked the depression right in the fucking eyeballs and he wrote about it. He talked about the progression and his recovery, and through it all he made it sound like something so relatable that I would be surprised if any reader didn't walk away with a better sense of how horrifying depression can be, even if it's nothing they have personally had to deal with themselves.<blockquote>What I had begun to discover is that, mysteriously and in ways that are totally remote from normal experience, the gray drizzle of horror induced by depression takes on the quality of physical pain. But it is not an immediately identifiable pain, like that of a broken limb. It may be more accurate to say that despair, owing to some evil trick played upon the sick brain by the inhabiting psyche, comes to resemble the diabolical discomfort of being imprisoned in a fiercely overheated room. And because no breeze stirs this caldron <i>[sic]</i>, because there is no escape from this smothering confinement, it is entirely natural that the victim begins to think ceaselessly of oblivion.<br />(p50)</blockquote>Styron's experiences may not be Every experience. If I have learned anything about depression is that it can be different for everybody, but that doesn't make any episode any less severe or important or worth discussing.<br /><br />I haven't always been perfect around those I love suffering from depression. I could always have been better, more patient, more understanding. No one can get it right 100% of the time. But we should continue to try. Reading Styron's memoir helps me remember that we can always do better, be better.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16904896118036445537" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_44944222" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/44944222" />
    <a title="Ade Bailey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/863801-ade-bailey"><img alt="Ade Bailey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1207383586p2/863801.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/44944222?book_show_action=true">Jan 31, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ade Bailey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ade Bailey" href="/user/show/863801-ade-bailey">Ade Bailey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/863801-ade-bailey?shelf=biography-autobiography">biography-autobiography</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/863801-ade-bailey?shelf=psychology">psychology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/863801-ade-bailey?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer44944222" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15026491856504922175">Revisited this. It is a literary gem as well as a marvellous compression into one short essay of that conceptually infinite beast called Depression. It came to mind after reading Thompson's <i>The City of Dreadful Night</i>, one of the greatest poetic descriptions of depression. Since I have at various levels been involved with depression throughout my life, I feel able to assert that the literary expression alone can come near to aiding understanding. My involvement at policy level with medical suppor</span>
  <span id="freeText15026491856504922175" style="display:none">Revisited this. It is a literary gem as well as a marvellous compression into one short essay of that conceptually infinite beast called Depression. It came to mind after reading Thompson's <i>The City of Dreadful Night</i>, one of the greatest poetic descriptions of depression. Since I have at various levels been involved with depression throughout my life, I feel able to assert that the literary expression alone can come near to aiding understanding. My involvement at policy level with medical support agencies left me in (not depressive) despair. <br /><br />"Depression', as Styron says is a mystery, intractable and deadly. Yet by using a single word it has become the plaything of virtually quack instant remedies, gross misunderstanding of its nature, and an alarming naming of misery or grief etc. as 'depression' with the concomitant dishing out of millions of drugs. What's going on in the total isolation of true depression is that <i>(i)n depression...faith in deliverance, in ultimate restoration, is absent. The pain is unrelenting, and what makes the condition intolerable is the foreknowledge that no remedy will come, not in a day, an hour, a month, or a minute. It is hopelessness even more than pain that crushes the soul.</i> We have to resort to a word such as 'soul'. <br /><br />I'll just pick out some aspects that Styron touches on, give them in very compressed form too. The beauty of the book is in the reading of it so go and read.<br /><br />It's always atypical, different for everybody, never fits in with what the technicians of the psyche with their charts and theories try to fit it into, although there is a relationship. It can begin slowly, with a lessening of pleasure. By the end it is a sense first of absolute numbness and loss of feeling, then a descent to what can only be, somewhat futilely, made analogous to great physical torment. It brings a sense of absolute isolation, losses of meaning, hope, love. Loss is central, a feeling of utter loss which Styron relates back to his pubescent childhood when his mother died. Styron also ponders whether ending a 40 year old affair with alcohol may have played a part. Stopping drinking was not willed; it was his body seemed to become highly sickened by alcohol. He would be one of many, perhaps the majority, of heavy drinkers who started drinking to alleviate anxiety and depression, or those whose minds were brought to depression by long term use.<br /><br />One of the hardest parts for Styron - and I have heard this over and over from so many people - was hiding his inner state from other people, trying to function socially and intellectually with the crushing weight inside.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15026491856504922175" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1697445783" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1697445783" />
    <a title="Sean-Wyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35732074-sean-wyn"><img alt="Sean-Wyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593924296p2/35732074.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1697445783?book_show_action=true">Jun 28, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sean-Wyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sean-Wyn" href="/user/show/35732074-sean-wyn">Sean-Wyn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35732074-sean-wyn?shelf=memoir">memoir</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1697445783" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15333993449416067339">When I was in 8th grade, one of my best friends developed the habit of crying every morning at school. She would throw her lunch away every single time we sat down to eat together at the cafeteria. At the time, I thought she was just being melodramatic; she had had a tendency towards that when she was younger.<br/><br/>But in high school, when I got a hint of what it was like to have "the blues," so to speak, I finally understood. Although what I went through at times was nowhere near as devastating as wh</span>
  <span id="freeText15333993449416067339" style="display:none">When I was in 8th grade, one of my best friends developed the habit of crying every morning at school. She would throw her lunch away every single time we sat down to eat together at the cafeteria. At the time, I thought she was just being melodramatic; she had had a tendency towards that when she was younger.<br /><br />But in high school, when I got a hint of what it was like to have "the blues," so to speak, I finally understood. Although what I went through at times was nowhere near as devastating as what Styron describes, I learned from my experience not to dismiss the pain of others so easily in the future.<br /><br />My dad told me this summer that he went through depression during his college years for three weeks. He lost twenty pounds and didn't even have the motivation to get up in the morning or do anything, pretty much. As I was reading <i>Darkness Visible</i>, I saw my dad's experience reflected through Styron's descriptions. What my dad couldn't articulate in English, what with it being his second language and all, Styron illustrated perfectly. I believe this is a very important book to get people to achieve an understanding of exactly what it's like to have depression, even if that understanding is limited. Hopefully with greater awareness of its effects, more suicides can be averted.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15333993449416067339" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('lH/2BDqMYnL9vEnIdVzEaKrueJrv6bNdmuxMUEZtqjPFFsZTeQYD1rqmDPW+UgCFk1umAD+J/jqmIUCHZ6Nvgg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  }

اجتاحت العالم في عشرينيات القرن الماضي. حققت هذه الرواية نجاحًا عالميًّا، تكرر في أعماله اللاحقة مثل «ذئب بين ذئاب» (1937) و«كلٌّ يموت من أجل نفسه فحسب» (1947).\nلم تفقد أعمال «فالادا» راهنيتها وأهميتها حتى اليوم، فما زالت تصدر في طبعات جديدة، وما زالت تُترجم وتُعاد ترجمتها إلى عديد من لغات العالم، كما تحول عدد كبير من أعماله إلى أفلام سينمائية وأعمال مسرحية.\nقضى «فالادا» فترات من حياته في       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/7565.William_Styron">About William Styron</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/7565.William_Styron"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1490146642p3/7565.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/7565.William_Styron">William Styron
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
671
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer7567865243126301157">William Styron (1925–2006), born in Newport News, Virginia, was one of the greatest American writers of his generation. Styron published his first book, <i>Lie Down in Darkness</i>, at age twenty-six and went on to write such influential works as the controversial and Pulitzer Prize–winning <i>The Confessions of Nat Turner</i> and the international bestseller <i>Sophie’s Choice</i>.</span>

</div>
</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/1258333-darkness-visible-a-memoir-of-madness">Quotes from Darkness Visible:...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;A phenomenon that a number of people have noted while in deep depression is the sense of being accompanied by a second self — a wraithlike observer who, not sharing the dementia of his double, is able to watch with dispassionate curiosity as his companion struggles against the oncoming disaster, or decides to embrace it. There is a theatrical quality about all this, and during the next several days, as I went about stolidly preparing for extinction, I couldn't shake off a sense of melodrama — a melodrama in which I, the victim-to-be of self-murder, was both the solitary actor and lone member of the audience.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/356873-a-phenomenon-that-a-number-of-people-have-noted-while">360 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;The pain of severe depression is quite unimaginable to those who have not suffered it, and it kills in many instances because its anguish can no longer be borne. The prevention of many suicides will continue to be hindered until there is a general awareness of the nature of this pain.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/926441-the-pain-of-severe-depression-is-quite-unimaginable-to-those">181 likes</a>
            </nobr>
